Divine Speech: One Attribute, Multiple Relations
Dardeer explains divine speech as one attribute with multiple relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine speech is a single eternal attribute that becomes multiple through its relations, and how it is divided into command, prohibition, news, and inquiry.

Core Idea
Divine speech is one in essence, multiple in relations.

This passage from Imam Dardeer's commentary tackles a subtle point in Islamic theology: how can the Qur'an contain commands, prohibitions, and narratives if God's speech is a single eternal attribute? Dardeer explains that the attribute of speech is one, but it relates to different objects, producing different categories. Let's explore how this works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms and the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the unity and multiplicity
Look for the statement that speech is one in itself and becomes multiple through relations. Compare to how knowledge and power are described.
Dardeer uses the analogy of knowledge and power to clarify that a single attribute can relate to many objects.
3
Understand the division into four categories
The passage divides speech into command, prohibition, news, and inquiry based on two dimensions: requirement of action/abandonment and affirmation/negation. Reflect on how each category arises from a different relation.
Command and prohibition relate to requiring action or abandonment; news and inquiry relate to affirming or negating something of something.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكلام يدل على ما ذكر دلالة مستمرة بلا انقطاع، أزلا وأبدا، فهو تعالى به آمر ناه مخبر، فهو في نفسه واحد، وتكثره إنما هو بتكثر التعلقات، كالعلم والقدرة. ولذا قسموه إلى أمر ونهي، وخبر واستخبار. فمن حيث اقتضاؤه فعلا أو تركا يسمى أمرا ونهيا، ومن حيث تعلقه بثبوت أمر لأمر، أو نفيه عنه، يسمى خبرا.
And speech indicates what was mentioned with a continuous indication without interruption, eternally and forever. So He, exalted, is by it commanding, forbidding, informing. So it is in itself one, and its multiplicity is only by the multiplicity of relations, like knowledge and power. Therefore they divided it into command, prohibition, news, and inquiry. For insofar as it necessitates action or abandonment, it is called command and prohibition; and insofar as it relates to the affirmation of something for something, or its negation, it is called news.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage focuses on the nature of divine speech (al-kalam). It states that speech indicates its objects continuously, without interruption, from eternity to eternity. Through speech, Allah is described as commanding, forbidding, and informing. However, the attribute of speech itself is one in essence; it becomes multiple only through the multiplicity of its relations (tacalluqat), similar to how knowledge and power are one attribute but relate to many objects. Based on these different relations, scholars have divided divine speech into command (amr), prohibition (nahy), news (khabar), and inquiry (istikhbar). Specifically, when speech relates to requiring an action or abandonment, it is called command or prohibition. When it relates to affirming something of something or negating it, it is called news. This preserves the unity of the divine attribute while accounting for the diversity of what it communicates.

Key Terms
الكلام
آمر
ناه
مخبر
تعدد التعلقات
أمر
نهي
خبر
استخبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think divine speech is multiple in essence, as if God has multiple distinct speech attributes.
Correction: Dardeer clarifies that speech is one in essence; its multiplicity comes only from the multiplicity of its relations, not from the essence itself.

Checkpoint
According to Dardeer, why is divine speech divided into command, prohibition, and news?
A) Because the essence of speech is multiple
B) Because of the multiplicity of its relations
C) Because it is created
D) Because it changes over time

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is a single eternal attribute that, through its various relations, appears as command, prohibition, news, and inquiry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a command of Allah, do you treat it as a temporary directive or as an expression of His eternal, unchanging speech? How might your obedience change if you saw each command as a direct relation of His one eternal attribute?
?
Consider a time you neglected a prohibition of Allah. Does recognizing that His prohibition is not a separate decree but a relation of His singular speech affect your sense of accountability? Will you commit to internalizing that His word is ever-present and unchanging?
?
The passage says divine speech indicates continuously without interruption. Do you live as if Allah is speaking to you now through His eternal speech? What would change if you truly believed His words are ever-present and actively relate to you at every moment?

Knowledge and Speech: Two Relations to Rational Judgments
Dardeer distinguishes the relation of knowledge and speech to rational judgments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinct relations of divine knowledge (disclosure) and divine speech (indication) to the three rational judgments, and why divine knowledge is not described as necessary or acquired.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge discloses; divine speech indicates.

In this passage, Imam Dardeer explains that although both divine knowledge and speech relate to all rational judgments, each does so in a fundamentally different way. Knowledge unveils the truth directly, while speech points to meanings. Furthermore, divine knowledge encompasses all things eternally without any process of reflection or inference, distinguishing it from human knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the key terms: علْم, كلام, and how they differ.
2
Identify the two relations
Notice that Dardeer states both attributes relate to all rational judgments, but their relation differs. Determine the specific words used for each relation.
Knowledge is 'انكشاف' (disclosure); speech is 'دلالة' (indication).
3
Understand the nature of divine knowledge
Dardeer elaborates that divine knowledge covers all universals and particulars eternally, without needing reflection or inference. Therefore, it is neither 'ضروري' (necessary) nor 'نظري' (acquired). Reflect on why this matters for theology.
This emphasizes that God's knowledge is unlike human knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعلقهما بجميع أقسام الحكم العقلي فظاهر، إلا أنَّ تعلَّقَها مُختلِفٌ، فتعلق العِلْمِ تعلق انكشاف، وتعلُّقُ الكَلامِ تَعلَّقُ دلالةٍ كما فُهِمَ لَمَّا ذَكَرْتُه لك. فالعلم يتعلق بجميع الكليات والجزئياتِ، أَزَلا وأبدًا، بلا تامل واستدلال، ولا سبَبٍ مِنَ الأسبابِ، فلا يوصَفُ بالضَّروري ولا بالنَّظَري، وله تعلق واحد تنجيزي قديم.
As for their relation to all divisions of the rational judgment, it is apparent, except that their relation differs. The relation of knowledge is a relation of disclosure, and the relation of speech is a relation of indication, as understood from what I mentioned to you. For knowledge relates to all universals and particulars, eternally and everlastingly, without reflection or inference, nor any cause from among causes; thus it is not described as necessary or acquired. And it has a single eternal actual relation.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines how the attributes of knowledge (علم) and speech (كلام) relate to the three rational judgments (واجب، مستحيل، جائز). The author states that both attributes relate to all judgments, but in different ways. Knowledge is a relation of disclosure (انكشاف), meaning it directly unveils the truth of things. Speech is a relation of indication (دلالة), meaning it points to meanings. Knowledge encompasses all universals and particulars eternally, without needing reflection or inference. Therefore, divine knowledge is not described as necessary (ضروري) — like sense perception — nor as acquired (نظري) — like reasoned conclusions. It has a single eternal actual relation (تعلق تنجيزي قديم), meaning it is always actively knowing.

Key Terms

كلام
حكم عقلي
تعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think divine knowledge is necessary (like sense perception) or acquired (like reasoned conclusions) similar to human knowledge.
Correction: The passage clarifies that divine knowledge is neither ضروري nor نظري; it is above all temporal processes, being eternal and all-encompassing without any need for reflection or inference.

Checkpoint
How is the relation of divine knowledge to rational judgments described?
A) Indication
B) Disclosure
C) Command
D) Prohibition

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge discloses all things eternally without process, unlike human knowledge, and is neither necessary nor acquired.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the truth that Allah's knowledge is not acquired through reflection or inference challenge your own understanding of God? Does your heart actually rest in the fact that He knows everything eternally, or do you subconsciously think He 'finds out' about your circumstances?
?
If divine speech indicates (دلالة) rather than discloses, how does that affect your relationship with the Qur'an? Are you content with the indication, or do you seek a direct disclosure that is not of this world?
?
The passage says Allah's knowledge is a single eternal act with no causes. Are there moments in your life when you act as if Allah is surprised by your choices, or as if He needs time to respond? How would your trust change if you truly believed His knowledge is timeless and causeless?

Does Knowledge Suffice for Hearing and Seeing?
Dardeer refutes the objection that knowledge alone suffices for hearing and seeing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the detailed mention of divine attributes like hearing and sight is necessary despite knowledge encompassing all existent things.

Core Idea
Detailed exposition of each attribute, not just coverage, is essential for creed.

This passage addresses a subtle objection: if Allah's knowledge covers everything, then why mention hearing and sight separately? Dardeer provides a concise answer that reveals a key principle in Islamic theology: the purpose of discussing attributes is to detail beliefs, not merely to cover conceptual ground. As you read, note how the objection is framed and how the answer shifts the focus from conceptual coverage to practical creedal detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the sharp contrast between the objection and the answer.
The objection is introduced with 'إن قيل' and the answer with 'أجيب'.
2
Understand the objection's logic
The objector argues: since knowledge covers all existent things, and hearing/sight only cover existent things, knowledge already includes their domain. Therefore mentioning them separately is redundant.
The objector assumes that covering the same domain makes the attributes equivalent.
3
Analyze the answer's core principle
Dardeer answers that the goal is to detail beliefs (ذكر العقائد مفصلة). This means each attribute must be explicitly stated in creed, even if conceptually subsumed under another.
The answer emphasizes completeness and explicitness in doctrinal formulation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قيل: إذا كان متعلق العلم شاملا لجميع أقسام الحكم العقلي، دخل فيه متعلق السمع والبصر لتعلقهما بالموجود فقط، فكان العلم يغني عن ذكر السمع والبصر ؟! أجيب بأن المطلوب ذكر العقائد مفصلة.
If it is said: When the object of knowledge encompasses all divisions of rational judgment, then the object of hearing and sight enters into it, since they relate only to existent things; so knowledge would suffice for mentioning hearing and sight?! The answer is: What is required is to mention beliefs in detail.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector argues: since knowledge covers all rational judgments, and hearing and sight only cover existent things, knowledge already covers their domain. So why mention hearing and sight separately? The answer: The purpose is to detail beliefs (العقائد) thoroughly, not to merely cover the domain. Even if knowledge encompasses all, each attribute has its distinct implications for belief.

Key Terms
علم
سمع
بصر
عقائد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if an attribute like knowledge is comprehensive, other attributes like hearing and sight are practically equivalent and need not be separately affirmed.
Correction: The answer clarifies that creedal detail requires explicit mention of each attribute, even if conceptually overlapping. The purpose is to provide a complete and precise statement of beliefs.

Checkpoint
Why does the author answer that knowledge does not suffice for mentioning hearing and sight?
A) Because hearing and sight are broader than knowledge
B) Because the goal is to detail beliefs
C) Because knowledge is not related to existent things
D) Because the objection is invalid

Key Takeaway
The detailed mention of each divine attribute, even if conceptually subsumed, is necessary for a complete and explicit statement of Islamic creed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever settle for a vague 'I believe in Allah' without taking time to learn and affirm each of His specific attributes as taught by the scholars? What might you lose by skipping that detail?
?
The objection says, 'Knowledge covers everything, so why list hearing and sight?' In your own faith, do you sometimes rely on general summaries rather than engaging with the specifics? What is one attribute of Allah that you have not deeply contemplated, and what would it take for you to focus on it?
?
If you were to write a personal creed based on this lesson, what specific attributes would you include, and why is it important for you to affirm each one explicitly rather than just assuming they are covered by a broader term?

Divine Speech: Unity in Essence, Multiplicity by Relations
Dardeer explains divine speech's unity and its divisions by relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine speech is one in essence and becomes multiple only through its relations to different contents, and how it is divided into command, prohibition, informative, and interrogative.

Core Idea
Divine speech is one essence, many relations.

In this passage, Imam Dardeer clarifies a key aspect of the divine attribute of speech. He emphasizes that God's speech is single in its essence but appears as multiple commands, prohibitions, and statements because of the different things it relates to. Understanding this requires distinguishing between the attribute itself and the objects it connects to. This lesson unpacks that distinction and the resulting classification of speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Let the author's words establish the framework before delving into commentary.
Notice the emphasis on continuity and eternity.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage states that speech is one in itself (واحد في نفسه) and its multiplicity comes from relations (تعلقات). Find this key sentence and note the analogy with knowledge and power.
3
Examine the four divisions
The text lists command (أمر), prohibition (نهي), informative (خبر), and interrogative (استخبار). Understand the basis for each: action/abandonment for command/prohibition, affirmation/negation for informative.
Interrogative is mentioned but not elaborated.
4
Reflect on the analogy
Consider why Dardeer compares speech to knowledge and power. How does this help explain unity with multiplicity?
This analogy reinforces the concept of attribute unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكلام يدل على ما ذُكِرَ دلالةً مُستمِرّةً بلا انقطاع ، أَزَلا وأبَدًا، فهو تعالى به آمر ناهِ مُخبر، فهو في نفْسِه واحِدٌ، وتكثُرُه إِنما هو بتكثر التعلقاتِ، كالعِلْم والقُدْرِة ولذا قسَّموه إلى أمْرٍ ونهي، وخبَرٍ واستخبارٍ . فمِنْ حيث اقتضاؤه فِعْلًا أو تركا يُسمّى أَمْرًا وَنهَيًا، ومِنْ حِيثُ تعَلَّقُهُ بِبُوتِ أَمرٍ لأمر، أو نفيه عنه، يُسمّى خبَرًا.
And speech indicates what was mentioned with a continuous indication without interruption, eternally and everlastingly. So He, exalted, is by it a commander, a prohibitor, an informer. It is in itself one, and its multiplicity is only due to the multiplicity of relations, like knowledge and power. Therefore, they divided it into command, prohibition, informative, and interrogative. So in terms of its requiring an action or abandonment, it is called command and prohibition. And in terms of its relation to the affirmation of something for something, or its negation from it, it is called informative.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines divine speech (كلام) and its divisions. Speech continuously indicates meanings eternally. Through it, God commands, prohibits, and informs. Speech is one in essence (واحد في نفسه) but becomes multiple due to different relations (تعلقات). This is similar to knowledge and power, which are one but have multiple objects. Scholars divided speech into command (أمر), prohibition (نهي), informative (خبر), and interrogative (استخبار). Command/prohibition relate to requiring an action or abandonment; informative relates to affirming or negating something about something. This shows that the division is based on the relation of speech to its content, not a division in the essence.

آمر
ناه
مخبر
أمر

خبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think divine speech is multiple in essence because there are different types like command and prohibition.
Correction: The author clarifies that speech is one in essence; multiplicity arises only from its relations (تعلقات) to different objects, not from a division in the attribute itself.

Checkpoint

A) Multiplicity in essence
B) Multiplicity by relations
C) Multiplicity by time
D) Multiplicity by place

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is a single eternal attribute that manifests as various types based on its relation to what it conveys.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat God's commands and prohibitions as if they come from separate authorities, or do you recognize them as flowing from His single eternal speech? How does this unity affect your obedience?
?
When you read the Qur'an, do you see it as a collection of scattered instructions, or as the manifestation of one attribute that calls you to respond as a whole? What would change in your heart if you truly believed every word comes from a single, undivided divine speech?
?
The passage says God's speech is one in essence but multiple by relations. If you internalized that every command, prohibition, and piece of news from God is rooted in the same eternal attribute, would you still pick and choose what to follow? Challenge yourself: Which part of God's speech do you resist, and how does that reveal a belief that it is not all from the same source?

The Dispute on Conditions for Divine Speech
Dardeer presents the dispute on conditions for divine speech and its link to rulings' temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement about whether divine speech requires actual addressees and how this affects the temporality of rulings.

Core Idea
Divine speech does not need actual addressees; God's knowledge suffices.

A key question in Islamic theology is whether divine commands and prohibitions require the actual existence of addressees. Dardeer presents a dispute on this issue and links it to the debate on whether rulings are temporal or eternal. This lesson unpacks that dispute and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the dispute
Begin by reading the Arabic passage about the dispute. Notice the question and the two possible positions.
The text says there is disagreement (خلاف).
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the view that requires actual addressees and the view that treats future addressees as present in God's knowledge.
3
Reflect on the implication for rulings
Understand how this dispute affects whether rulings (الأحكام) are considered temporal or eternal.
The key is God's knowledge of future existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل يُشترط في تسميته بذلك كالخطاب وُجود مخاطبين بالفعل أو لا؟ خلاف، وينبني عليه الخلاف في الأحكام، هل هي حادثة أو قديمة باعتبار تنزيل من سيُوجَدُ منزِلَةَ الموجود؛ اكتفاء بوجودِ المأمورِ فِي عِلْمِ الآمرِ. وله تعلَّقات ثلاثةُ: تنجيزي قديم باعتبار دلالته على الواجبات والمستحيلات والجائزات، التي سيوجَدُ منها وما لا يوجَدُ، و صلاحي قديم باعتبار دلالته على الأمر والنهي قبلَ وُجودِ المخاطبين، و تنجيزي حادثٌ عِندَ وُجودِهم.
And is it required for its being named as such — like a discourse — the actual existence of addressees or not? There is disagreement, and upon it is built the disagreement concerning the rulings: whether they are temporal or eternal, on account of treating one who will exist as if existing, being satisfied with the existence of the commanded in the knowledge of the commander. And it has three relations: an eternal actual relation, on account of its indication of the necessary, impossible, and possible things — those which will come to exist and those which will not; and an eternal potential relation, on account of its indication of command and prohibition before the existence of addressees; and a temporal actual relation at the time of their existence.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage examines a scholarly dispute about divine speech: does calling it 'command' or 'prohibition' require the actual existence of addressees? The author notes that this dispute affects whether the rulings (الأحكام) are temporal or eternal. Some argue that God treats future addressees as if present, because He knows they will exist. Then the author lists three relations (تعلقات) of speech: 1) Eternal actual (تنجيزي قديم): speech indicates necessary, impossible, and possible things eternally, regardless of actualization. 2) Eternal potential (صلاحي قديم): speech indicates command and prohibition before addressees exist, i.e., it is potentially applicable. 3) Temporal actual (تنجيزي حادث): at the moment addressees exist, speech relates to them actually. This classification clarifies that divine speech is eternal but its specific relations may be temporal based on context.

Key Terms
تسمية
خطاب
مخاطب
حكم
حادثة
قديمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think divine commands are only meaningful when addressed to existing people, so they must be temporal.
Correction: The text shows that God's knowledge of future addressees suffices, allowing the rulings to be eternal in His knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the dispute about?
A) Whether divine speech requires actual addressees
B) Whether divine speech is created
C) Whether divine speech has letters
D) Whether divine speech is a single attribute

Key Takeaway
The temporality of divine rulings hinges on whether one requires actual addressees or accepts God's knowledge of future existence as sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of your future existence is sufficient for His commands to be real and binding, how seriously do you take His commands in the present moment? Do you live as if you are already standing before Him?
?
The text shows that God treats future beings as present due to His knowledge. How does this affect your trust in God's plan for your life, even when you cannot see how it will unfold?
?
One view holds that rulings are temporal, coming into existence when you need them. The other says they are eternal in God's knowledge. Which view makes you more conscious that God's commands are not arbitrary but grounded in His eternal wisdom? How does that shape your obedience today?

The Three Relations of Divine Speech
Dardeer lists the three relations of divine speech with their descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the three relations of divine speech: eternal actual, eternal potential, and temporal actual.

Core Idea
Divine speech has both eternal and temporal aspects in its relations.

After addressing the dispute on addressees, Dardeer clarifies that divine speech has three distinct relations. These relations explain how the eternal attribute of speech connects to created realities. In this lesson, we explore these three categories and their meanings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three relations
Read the Arabic passage listing the three relations. Notice the three distinct terms: تنجيزي قديم, صلاحي قديم, and تنجيزي حادث.
The terms are technical and will be explained.
2
Understand each relation
For each relation, identify what it indicates and when it applies. The first two are eternal, the third is temporal.
Eternal actual (تنجيزي قديم) indicates necessary, impossible, and possible things eternally.
3
Compare the relations
Compare the eternal potential (صلاحي قديم) with the temporal actual (تنجيزي حادث). The former is before addressees exist, the latter at their existence.
4
Consider the significance
Reflect on why this classification matters: it preserves the eternity of speech while allowing for temporal expressions.
This resolves apparent tensions in divine speech theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل يُشترط في تسميته بذلك كالخطاب وُجود مخاطبين بالفعل أو لا؟ خلاف، وينبني عليه الخلاف في الأحكام، هل هي حادثة أو قديمة باعتبار تنزيل من سيُوجَدُ منزِلَةَ الموجود؛ اكتفاء بوجودِ المأمورِ فِي عِلْمِ الآمرِ. وله تعلَّقات ثلاثةُ: تنجيزي قديم باعتبار دلالته على الواجبات والمستحيلات والجائزات، التي سيوجَدُ منها وما لا يوجَدُ، و صلاحي قديم باعتبار دلالته على الأمر والنهي قبلَ وُجودِ المخاطبين، و تنجيزي حادثٌ عِندَ وُجودِهم.
And is it required for its being named as such — like a discourse — the actual existence of addressees or not? There is disagreement, and upon it is built the disagreement concerning the rulings: whether they are temporal or eternal, on account of treating one who will exist as if existing, being satisfied with the existence of the commanded in the knowledge of the commander. And it has three relations: an eternal actual relation, on account of its indication of the necessary, impossible, and possible things — those which will come to exist and those which will not; and an eternal potential relation, on account of its indication of command and prohibition before the existence of addressees; and a temporal actual relation at the time of their existence.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage examines a scholarly dispute about divine speech: does calling it 'command' or 'prohibition' require the actual existence of addressees? The author notes that this dispute affects whether the rulings (الأحكام) are temporal or eternal. Some argue that God treats future addressees as if present, because He knows they will exist. Then the author lists three relations (تعلقات) of speech: 1) Eternal actual (تنجيزي قديم): speech indicates necessary, impossible, and possible things eternally, regardless of actualization. 2) Eternal potential (صلاحي قديم): speech indicates command and prohibition before addressees exist, i.e., it is potentially applicable. 3) Temporal actual (تنجيزي حادث): at the moment addressees exist, speech relates to them actually. This classification clarifies that divine speech is eternal but its specific relations may be temporal based on context.

Key Terms
تعلق
تنجيزي قديم
صلاحي قديم
تنجيزي حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because speech is eternal, all its aspects must be eternal, including its application to specific commands.
Correction: The third relation shows that actual commands can be temporal, even though the speech attribute itself is eternal.

Checkpoint
Which relation of divine speech occurs only when addressees actually exist?
A) Eternal actual
B) Eternal potential
C) Temporal actual
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The three relations show how an eternal attribute of speech can have temporal applications without compromising its eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Do you live as if God's speech only matters when you read it, or does the reality of its eternal actual relation (covering all possible and impossible things) cause you to humble yourself before the One whose knowledge and speech encompass everything—including your hidden secrets?
?
The passage states that divine speech has an eternal actual relation covering even things that will never exist. How should this affect your confidence in God's decrees over your life, especially when you don't understand His planning? Can you trust that His speech about your future is already settled in eternity?

Dardeer states that power and will encompass all possible things, refuting Mu'tazila

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Divine power and will cover every possible thing—including human actions.

This lesson introduces the second category of divine attributes: those that relate to all possible things. Imam Dardeer, in his poem and commentary, clearly states that power and will encompass every possible entity, and he directly refutes the Mu'tazila who limit divine power by excluding human voluntary actions. Let's read the passage and unpack its meaning step by step.

Guided Path
1

Possible things are those that can exist or not, without contradiction.
3

This is a major sectarian disagreement about divine omnipotence and human free will.
4

The author uses the word 'all' (كلها) and the address 'O brother of piety' to emphasize that every possible thing—including human actions—is under divine power and will. This directly refutes the Mu'tazila limitation.
The phrase 'أخا التقى' draws attention and affirms the inclusive scope.

This passage affirms that absolute divine power and will are not restricted. Human actions, being possible, are within God's control, refuting any claim of human independence.
This aligns with Sunni Aqeedah that Allah creates all actions, including those of humans.

The Three Relations of Divine Speech
Defines the dispute on addressees and enumerates the three ta'alluq of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute about whether divine speech requires actual addressees, and the three types of relations (ta'alluq) that speech has: eternal determinative, eternal potential, and temporal determinative.

Core Idea
Divine speech has both eternal and temporal aspects, not only temporal.

This passage from Imam Dardir's commentary tackles a foundational issue in Islamic theology: how do we speak of God's speech when there were no creatures? The author first presents a scholarly dispute, then clarifies that divine speech has three distinct relations — eternal and temporal. Understanding these relations is key to grasping how God's attributes relate to creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a dispute.
2
Identify the dispute
The passage presents a disagreement: is it necessary for actual addressees to exist for God's speech to be called 'speech'? Find this dispute and note its implication for whether rulings are temporal or eternal.
The author implies a resolution by mentioning God's knowledge.
3
Find the three relations
The passage lists three ta'alluq of speech. Locate each one: eternal determinative, eternal potential, and temporal determinative. Understand how each relates to time and to the content of speech.
The terms tanzīzī qadīm, sulūhī qadīm, tanzīzī ḥādith are key.
4
Understand the categories indicated
The eternal determinative relation indicates the necessary, impossible, and contingent. Reflect on why these categories are eternal and how they relate to God's knowledge.
This shows that speech is not limited to temporal commands.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the author enumerates these three relations. How does this classification help resolve the dispute about temporal versus eternal rulings?
This connects to the broader issue of divine attributes and their relationship with creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الأول: الإلهيات وهل يُشترط في تسميته بذلك كالخطاب وُجود مخاطبين بالفعل أو لا؟ خلاف، وينبني عليه الخلاف في الأحكام، هل هي حادثة أو قديمة باعتبار تنزيل من سيُوجَدُ منزِلَةَ الموجود؛ اكتفاء بوجودِ المأمورِ فِي عِلْمِ الآمرِ. وله تعلَّقات ثلاثة: تنجيزي قديم باعتبار دلالته على الواجبات والمستحيلات والجائزات، التي سيوجَدُ منها وما لا يوجَدُ، وصلوحي قديم باعتبار دلالته على الأمر والنهي قبلَ وُجودِ المخاطبين، وتنجيزي حادثٌ عِندَ وُجودِهم.
The first section: Divine matters. And is it required in naming it (i.e., speech) as such, like address, the actual existence of addressees or not? There is a dispute, and upon it is built the dispute concerning the rulings: whether they are temporal or eternal, by considering the one who will exist as having the status of the existent, due to sufficiency of the existence of the commanded in the knowledge of the commander. And for it (speech) there are three relations: an eternal determinative (tanzīzī qadīm) by virtue of its indication of the necessaries, impossibles, and contingents, which will exist and those which will not exist; and an eternal potential (sulūhī qadīm) by virtue of its indication of command and prohibition before the existence of the addressees; and a temporal determinative (tanzīzī ḥādith) at the time of their existence.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the divine attribute of speech and its relations. It first presents a dispute: is the term 'speech' applied to God only if there are actual addressees? The dispute affects whether the rulings (ahkām) are temporal or eternal. The author implies the correct view by noting that future beings are considered as if existing because God knows them. Then he enumerates three ta'alluq of speech: (1) an eternal determinative relation – speech indicates what is necessary, impossible, and possible from eternity, regardless of occurrence; (2) an eternal potential relation – speech indicates command and prohibition before any addressees exist; (3) a temporal determinative relation – at the moment addressees exist. This shows speech has both eternal and temporal aspects.

جائزات

Correction: The passage clarifies that speech has two eternal relations (determinative and potential) in addition to a temporal one.

Checkpoint

B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
Divine speech has three relations: an eternal determinative (indicating necessary/impossible/contingent), an eternal potential (command/prohibition before addressees), and a temporal determinative (at the time of addressees).

Power and Will: Refutation of the Mu'tazila
Introduces power and will and refutes Mu'tazila on human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's power and will encompass all contingent beings, and why the Mu'tazila view that humans independently create their actions is rejected as a corrupt doctrine.

Core Idea
Allah's power and will extend to everything possible, including human actions.

This passage marks a transition to discussing two more divine attributes: power and will. It clarifies their scope—they relate to all contingent beings—and directly confronts the Mu'tazila claim that humans create their own actions independently. The author emphasizes that denying this fundamental truth undermines true piety.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an initial sense of the content. Notice the address 'O brother of piety' as a rhetorical device.
2
Identify the two attributes and their scope
The passage states that power and will relate to all contingent beings, not to necessary beings or impossibles. Understand what 'contingent', 'necessary', and 'impossible' mean in this context.
Contingents are things that can exist or not exist; necessary beings are those that must exist (like Allah); impossibles are those that cannot exist.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila claim that God's power does not apply to human voluntary actions—humans independently create their own actions. They also say sins are not willed by Allah because for them will entails command.
This is a key point of contention in Islamic theology.
4
Reflect on the author's response
The author strongly refutes this, calling it a corrupt doctrine and declaring that one who does not affirm the correct view is not truly pious. Consider why this is such a serious matter in creed.
The address 'O brother of piety' is used to underscore that true piety requires this belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني: ما يتعَلَّقُ بجميع المُمْكِناتِ، وهو صفتان أيضًا، القُدرة والإرادة، وإليه أشار بقوله: وقدرة، إرادة تعلّقاً * بالممكنات كلها أخا التقى وقُدرة، إرادة تعلقاً بالمُمْكِنات لا بالواجبات ولا بالمستحيلات. وأشار بقوله كلها يا أخا التقى أي: يا أيُّها الملازم على التقوى، للرَّدِّ على المعتزلة القائلين بأنَّ قُدْرتَه تعالى لا تتعلَّق بأفعال العبدِ الاختيارية، بل العبدُ مُستَقِل بخلق فعله الاختياري، وأن بعض أفعاله الاختياريّةِ كالمعاصي ليست بإرادة الله تعالى؛ بناءً على أنَّ الإرادة تستلزم الأمر، أو هي عينه. ولا ريب في أنه مذهب فاسد ومِنْ ثَمَّ أَشرتُ بقَولي «أخا التقى» إلى أَنَّ مَنْ لَمْ يَعتَقِدْ ما قُلْنا فَلَيسَ بِتَقيِّ.
The second section: what pertains to all contingent beings, and it is also two attributes: power and will. And to it he alluded by his saying: 'And power, will, are related to all contingents, O brother of piety.' And power and will are related to contingents, not to necessary beings nor to impossibles. And he alluded by his saying 'all of them, O brother of piety' meaning: O you who adhere to piety, as a refutation of the Mu'tazila who say that His power, exalted, is not related to the voluntary actions of the servant, rather the servant is independent in creating his voluntary action, and that some of his voluntary actions, like sins, are not by the will of God, based on the principle that will necessitates command or is the same as it. Commentary
This passage introduces the second major category of divine attributes: those related to all contingent beings, namely power (qudra) and will (irāda). It first states that these two attributes relate to all contingents (mumkināt), not to necessary beings (wājibāt) or impossibles (mustaḥīlāt). The author quotes the verse line from al-Kharīda and explains it: 'O brother of piety' is a call to the one who adheres to God-consciousness, and it serves as a refutation of the Mu'tazila. The Mu'tazila deny that God's power extends to the voluntary actions of humans; instead, they claim humans independently create their own actions. Furthermore, they claim that sinful acts are not willed by God, because for them will necessitates command or is identical to it. The author strongly refutes this, calling it a corrupt doctrine, and declares that one who does not affirm this is not truly pious.

The Three Relations of Divine Will
Classifies will's relations into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct relations of divine will—two eternal and one temporal—and how they describe God's specification of possibilities.

Core Idea
God's will specifies possibilities eternally and temporally.

In this passage, Imam al-Dardīr unpacks the special relation of divine will to contingent beings. While both power and will relate to contingents, will has a unique role: it specifies which possibilities become actual. He then classifies this relation into two eternal types and a third temporal one, giving us a precise vocabulary for how God's eternal decree relates to temporal events.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key terms: 'takhṣīṣ', 'tanzīzī qadīm', 'ṣulūḥī qadīm', and 'tanzīzī ḥādith'.
The passage uses a compact style typical of kalām texts.
2
Identify the two eternal relations
Find where the passage describes 'tanzīzī qadīm' and 'ṣulūḥī qadīm'. Understand that the first is the eternal determination of how things will be, and the second is the eternal potential for them to be otherwise.
These are both non-temporal aspects of will.
3
Note the third temporal relation
Look at the phrase 'qīla' (it is said) introducing a third relation. This is the temporal actualization of the eternal decree at the moment of creation.
Some scholars debate whether this is a distinct relation or a temporal aspect of the eternal one.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why does this classification matter? It emphasizes that God's will is not just a momentary choice but involves eternal determination and potentiality, with temporal execution.
This preserves both God's immutability and His active involvement in creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهُما وإنْ تعَلَّقاً بالممكن إلا أنَّ تعلَّقَ الإرادة به تعلَّق مخصوص، إذ هي صفةٌ تُخصِّصُ الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه، ولها تعلقان قديمان: تنجيزي وصلوحي: فتخصيصها في الأزَل الأشياء على الوجه الذي ستوجَدَ عليه فيما لا يزال تنجيزي قديمٌ، وصُلوحُها لأنْ يكونَ على خِلافِ ما هو عليه، صلوحي قديم. قيل: ولها تعلق ثالث، تنجيزي حادث، وهو : تخصيصها الشَّيءَ بالفعل وقتَ وُجودِه عَلَى وَفق التخصيص الأزلي.
And these two (power and will), although they are related to the contingent, yet the relation of will to it is a specific relation, for it is an attribute that particularizes the contingent with some of what is possible for it. And it has two eternal relations: determinative (tanzīzī) and potential (sulūhī). So its particularization of things in eternity in the way they will exist in the future is an eternal determinative relation. And its potentiality to be contrary to what it is upon is an eternal potential relation. It is said: And it has a third relation, a temporal determinative relation, which is its particularization of the thing to actual existence at the time of its existence, in accordance with the eternal particularization.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed analysis of the divine attribute of will (irāda). It begins by noting that while both power and will relate to contingent beings, will has a special relation: it specifies which possibilities become actual. The author then classifies the relations of will into two eternal types: one is the eternal determinative relation, which is God's eternal decision of how things will be; the other is the eternal potential relation, which is the fact that things could have been otherwise (if God had willed). Then he mentions a third relation, reported as 'qīla' (it is said), which is a temporal determinative relation: the actual bringing into existence of a thing at its appointed time, in accordance with the eternal decree. This third relation is sometimes debated, as some consider it merely a temporal aspect of the same eternal decree.

Key Terms
تخصيص
تنجيزي قديم
صلوحي قديم
تنجيزي حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think divine will only has a temporal relation, i.e., God wills things only at the moment of creation.
Correction: The passage shows that will has two eternal relations (determinative and potential) in addition to the temporal one, preserving God's eternal decree and power.

Checkpoint
How many ta'alluq of divine will are mentioned in the passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
Divine will specifies possibilities through three relations: two eternal (determinative and potential) and one temporal (executing the decree in time).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you truly believe that it was decreed by God's eternal determinative will before time began? How does that shape your patience and trust in His wisdom?
?
The passage teaches that God's will has a potential relation—things could have been different. How does this truth increase your hope in His mercy and your awe of His absolute power?
?
Recognising that every moment is an execution of an eternal decree, what changes in your daily awareness? Will you begin your day with the conviction that nothing happens outside His will?

The Two Relations of Divine Power and Their Detailed Enumeration
Enumerates the two relations of divine power and their sevenfold detail.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the eternal potential and temporal determinative relations of divine power, and the sevenfold detailed classification of how power relates to contingent beings.

Core Idea
Divine power has an eternal readiness and a temporal action, covering all states of creation and annihilation.

This passage from Imam Dardir's commentary delves into the attribute of divine power (qudra), explaining not only its two fundamental relations but also a nuanced seven-part breakdown. Understanding this structure helps clarify how God's power encompasses all possible states of contingent beings, from pre-existence through existence and beyond.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the text. Notice the key terms like صلوحي قديم and تنجيزي حادث.
The passage contains enumerations; pay attention to the numbering.
2
Identify the two main relations
The passage first states that power relates as either bringing into existence or annihilating, in accordance with will. Then it says there are two relations: eternal potential and temporal determinative. Make sure you understand the difference.
Eternal potential (sulūhī qadīm) is the readiness; temporal determinative (tanzīzī ḥādith) is the actual act.
3
Understand the link to Quranic terms
Notice that the temporal determinative relation is expressed as creation, provision, and giving life. These are the Quranic terms for God's actual intervention.
4
Study the sevenfold breakdown
Read through the seven enumerated items. They cover before existence, at existence, after existence, and at resurrection. Observe how each item relates to either the potential or determinative relation.
Items 1 and 2 are more about potential; items 3,5,7 are determinative acts; items 4 and 6 describe states under power.
5
Consider the possibility of an eighth division
The footnote suggests an eighth aspect: that a possible thing capable of non-existence never leaves the grasp of power. Reflect on why this might be added.
This shows the comprehensiveness of divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعلُّقُ القُدْرَةِ به فتعلُّقُ إيجاد أو إعدام على طبق الإرادة، ولها تعلُّقانِ: صلوحي قديم، وتنجيزي حادث، وهذا التعلُّق هو المعبّر عنه بالخلق والرزق والإحياء. تفصيله: ١. صلوحي قديم وهو صلاحيتها في الأزل للإيجاد والإعدام. ٢. كون الممكن فيما لا يزال قبل وجوده في قبضة القدرة إن شاء أبقاه على عدمه وإن شاء أوجده. ٣. إيجاده تعالى بها الأشياء فيما لا يزال وهذا من أقسام التعلق التنجيزي الحادث. ٤. كون الممكن حال وجوده في قبضة القدرة إن شاء أبقاه وإن شاء أعدمه. ٥. إعدام الله تعالى الأشياء بها وهذا أيضًا من أقسام التنجيزي الحادث. ٦. كون الممكن في حالة عدمه (أي بعد وجوده) في قبضة القدرة إن شاء أبقاه على عدمه وإن شاء أوجده. ٧. إيجاد الله تعالى الأشياء بها عند البعث. هذا ويمكن أن تكون الأقسام ثمانية بقطع النظر عيني [؟] أمر ممكن قابل للعدم لا يخرج عن قبضة القدرة أيضًا.
As for the relation of power to it (the contingent), it is a relation of bringing into existence or annihilating in accordance with will. And it has two relations: an eternal potential (sulūhī qadīm) and a temporal determinative (tanzīzī ḥādith). And this relation is what is expressed as creation (khalq), provision (rizq), and giving life (iḥyā'). Its breakdown: 1. Eternal potential: its (power's) suitability in eternity to bring into existence and annihilate. 2. The contingent, in the future before its existence, is in the grasp of power; if He wills He keeps it in non-existence, and if He wills He brings it into existence. 3. His bringing things into existence by it in the future; this is part of the temporal determinative relation. 4. The contingent, at the time of its existence, is in the grasp of power; if He wills He keeps it, and if He wills He annihilates it. 5. His annihilation of things by it; this is also part of the temporal determinative. 6. The contingent, in the state of its non-existence (i.e., after its existence) is in the grasp of power; if He wills He keeps it in non-existence, and if He wills He brings it into existence. 7. His bringing things into existence by it at the resurrection. This, and it is possible that the divisions are eight, disregarding the [?] that a possible thing capable of non-existence does not leave the grasp of power as well.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed analysis of the divine attribute of power (qudra). It first states that power is related to bringing into existence or annihilating, in accordance with the divine will. Power has two main relations: one eternal potential (sulūhī qadīm) – meaning power's ability to create or annihilate is eternal, even if not exercised; and one temporal determinative (tanzīzī ḥādith) – the actual act of creating or annihilating occurs in time. These actual acts are referred to as creation (khalq), provision (rizq), and giving life (iḥyā') in the Quran. The passage then elaborates a sevenfold (or possibly eightfold) detailed breakdown of these relations, including: (1) the eternal potentiality; (2) the state of a possible thing before existence being under divine power; (3) God's actual bringing it into existence in time; (4) the state of a thing during its existence still being under power (able to be kept or annihilated); (5) God's actual annihilation; (6) the state after annihilation still under power (able to be brought back); and (7) God's bringing back at resurrection. The footnote adds that it could be eight aspects if one considers that a possible thing never escapes divine power.

The Eternal Attachments of Divine Will
Dardeer defines divine will and its two eternal attachments, with a reported third.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine will attaches to possibles through two eternal modalities: existential and suitability, and the reported temporal attachment.

Core Idea
God's will eternally specifies each possible in two ways: decree and suitability.

This passage by Imam Dardeer unpacks the nature of divine will (irada). It distinguishes will from other attributes by its specifying function and then reveals the structure of its attachment to possibles. You'll encounter two eternal aspects—existential and suitability—and a third temporal one mentioned by some scholars. Let's read the source closely.

Read the Arabic passage

The passage is dense; take your time with each term.

Identify the two eternal attachments

3
Understand the reported third attachment
Dardeer mentions a third, temporal existential attachment reported by some. Reflect on why this is considered temporal and how it relates to the eternal ones.
The temporal attachment is the actualization of the eternal decree.

وهما وإن تعلقا بالممكن إلا أن تعلق الإرادة به تعلق مخصوص، إذ هي صفة تخصص الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه، ولها تعلقان قديمان: تنجيزي وصلوحي: فتخصيصها في الأزل الأشياء على الوجه الذي ستوجد عليه فيما لا يزال تنجيزي قديم، وصلوحها لأن يكون على خلاف ما هو عليه، صلوحي قديم. قيل: ولها تعلق ثالث، تنجيزي حادث، وهو: تخصيصها الشيء بالفعل وقت وجوده على وفق التخصيص الأزلي.
And although they both attach to the possible, the attachment of will to it is a specific attachment, for it is an attribute that specifies the possible with some of what is permissible for it. It has two eternal attachments: existential and suitability. So its specification in pre-eternity of things in the manner in which they will exist in what is to come is an eternal existential [attachment], and its suitability to be contrary to what it is is an eternal suitability [attachment]. It is said: it has a third attachment, a temporal existential [attachment], which is its specification of the thing by actual [existence] at the time of its existence in accordance with the pre-eternal specification.

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of will (irada) and explains its attachment to possibles. It first states that will, like power, attaches to possibles, but its attachment is specific: it is a specifying attribute. Then it enumerates two eternal attachments: (1) existential (tanzizi) – God's eternal decree of how things will be; (2) suitability (suluhi) – the eternal suitability of the opposite. Then it mentions a reported third attachment: temporal existential – the actual bringing into existence at the moment of creation, in accordance with the eternal decree.

The Attachment of Divine Power to Possibles
Dardeer defines divine power's two attachments and identifies them as attributes of actions.

Core Idea
Divine power connects to possibles through two attachments: one eternal in readiness, one temporal in action.

Dardeer now explains how divine power relates to possible things. The same power that enables the necessary aims at what is possible. But its relation is not one-dimensional. God's power has a timeless capacity and a moment-by-moment actuality. This distinction is crucial for understanding how God creates and sustains the universe without change in His essence.

Enumeration of seven attachments of divine power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven specific attachments of divine power as enumerated by Imam Dardeer and how they relate to the two main categories of suitability and existential.

Core Idea
Divine power attaches to possibilities in seven definite ways.

In the previous lesson, power's attachments were summarized into two categories: suitability and existential. Now the commentary unpacks each attachment in detail, enumerating seven distinct relations that divine power has to created things — from pre-eternal suitability to the resurrection. This lesson walks you through each attachment, its place in the timeline of a possible thing, and the distinction between eternal and temporal attachments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation

3
Trace the timeline of a possible thing
Attachments 2–7 follow the lifecycle of a possible being: before existence (#2), at the moment of creation (#3), during existence (#4), at the moment of destruction (#5), after existence (#6), and at resurrection (#7).
This shows that power is continuously related to the possible.
4

This shows scholarly precision and awareness of alternative ways to count.

The Hierarchy of Divine Power, Will, and Knowledge
Dardeer explains the logical order among power, will, and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical order among God's attributes of power, will, and knowledge, and why power depends on will which depends on knowledge.

Core Idea
God's power follows His will, and His will follows His knowledge.

In this passage, Imam Dardeer clarifies the relationship between three key divine attributes: knowledge, will, and power. He explains that they are not independent but follow a logical sequence. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for grasping how God's actions are both free and consistent with His foreknowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Note the key terms: تعلق (attachment), ترتب (ordered), and تابع (dependent).
This passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the ordering
Identify the three attributes: power (قدرة), will (إرادة), knowledge (علم). Find the statement that power depends on will, and will depends on knowledge.
The order is not temporal but logical.
3
Understand the implication
Reflect on the chain: God only brings into existence what He wills, and He only wills what He knows. This shows that God's actions are not arbitrary but based on His knowledge and will.
This connects to the topic of qadar (divine decree).
4
Analyze the example
The example given is the case of someone whom God knows will persist in disbelief until death. God does not will faith for him, so faith does not occur, even if God commands it. This illustrates the dependency chain.
This example helps to understand that what God commands (like belief) may not occur if He does not will it.
5
Summarize the significance
Conclude that the hierarchy protects against thinking that God's power operates independently of His wisdom and knowledge. It also explains why some people remain disbelievers despite God's command to believe.
This is a key doctrine in Sunni theology regarding divine attributes and predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم: أن تعلق القدرة والإرادة والعلم مترتب، فتعلق القدرة تابع لتعلق الإرادة، وتعلق الإرادة تابع لتعلق العلم، فلا يوجد شيئًا أو يعدمه إلا إذا أراده، ولا يريده إلا إذا علمه، فما علم أنه يكون أراد كونه، ثم أبرزه على طبق الإرادة، وما علم أنه لا يكون فلم يرد كونه، فلم يوجد وإن أمر به، كالإيمان ممن علم الله أنه يستمر على الكفر حتى الموت.
Know that the attachment of power, will, and knowledge is ordered. The attachment of power is dependent on the attachment of will, and the attachment of will is dependent on the attachment of knowledge. So He does not bring anything into existence or annihilate it unless He wills it, and He does not will it unless He knows it. Thus, that which He knows will be, He wills its existence, then brings it forth in accordance with will. And that which He knows will not be, He does not will its existence, so it does not exist, even if He commands it, like faith for someone whom God knows will persist in disbelief until death.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage explains the logical order among God's attributes of power, will, and knowledge in their attachment to things. The order is not temporal but intellectual: power follows will, and will follows knowledge. Thus, God's actions are based on His will, which is based on His foreknowledge. The example given is the case of a person whose disbelief until death is known by God: God does not will faith for him, so faith does not occur, even though God may command it.

إرادة

قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's will is independent of His knowledge, and that He wills arbitrarily before knowing.
Correction: The passage shows that will is dependent on knowledge: God does not will anything unless He knows it. So His will is always informed by His perfect knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) Knowledge
B) Power
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
God's power is dependent on His will, and His will is dependent on His knowledge; thus, nothing exists without being willed and known by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Are you assuming that because you have faith, you deserved it? Or are you overwhelmed with gratitude that He willed it for you—and terrified of what it means if He did not?

Why Power and Will Do Not Attach to the Necessary and Impossible
Dardeer argues that power and will cannot attach to wajib and mustahil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine power and will cannot logically attach to the necessary (wajib) and the impossible (mustahil), and why this is a perfection rather than a limitation.

Core Idea
Power and will only attach to what can receive existence after non-existence.

This passage tackles a common question: if God is all-powerful, why does His power not extend to everything? Imam Dardeer explains that power and will are attributes of effect, and an effect must come into existence after non-existence. The necessary (God) never was non-existent, and the impossible never can exist, so they cannot be objects of power or will. This is not a limitation but a perfection, as attaching them to the necessary or impossible would lead to logical absurdities.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Try to grasp the flow of Dardeer's argument.
Notice the concise style; each phrase carries weight.
2
Understand the key terms
Focus on 'wajib' (necessary), 'mustahil' (impossible), 'tahsil al-hasil' (attaining the already attained), and 'qalb al-haqa'iq' (inversion of realities). These are central to the argument.
3
Follow the logical argument
Trace why power and will cannot attach to wajib and mustahil. The argument is: power and will are attributes of effect; effect requires existence after non-existence; wajib never had non-existence; mustahil never accepts existence; therefore attaching them is impossible.
Identify the consequence: tahsil al-hasil and qalb al-haqa'iq.
4
Reflect on the perfection aspect
Consider why this is 'absolute perfection' and attaching would be 'deficiency beyond which there is no deficiency'. Reflect on the idea that God's perfection includes logical consistency.
This guards against anthropomorphic views of divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنها لم تتعلق القدرة والإرادة بالواجب والمستحيل؛ لأنهما لما كانا صفتي تأثير، ومن لازم الأثر وجوده بعد عدم، لزم أن ما لم يقبل العدم أصلا، وهو الواجب، وما يقبل الوجود أصلا، وهو المستحيل، لم يصح أن يكون أثرا لها، وإلا لزم تحصيل الحاصل وقلب الحقائق بصيرورة الواجب أو المستحيل جائزا، وهو تهافت لا يعقل. فالكمال المطلق في عدم تعلقهما بالواجب والمستحيل؛ لما علمت، والنقص الذي ما بعده نقص تعلقها بهما، المؤدي ذلك إلى إعدامهما أنفسهما وإعدام الذات العلية وإيجاد الشريك والعجز والجهل.
And indeed, power and will do not attach to the necessary and the impossible, because since they are attributes of effect, and necessary to the effect is its existence after non-existence, it follows that that which does not accept non-existence at all – which is the necessary – and that which accepts existence at all – which is the impossible – cannot be an effect of them. Otherwise, it would necessitate the attainment of the already attained and the inversion of realities by the necessary or the impossible becoming possible, which is an incomprehensible incoherence. Thus, absolute perfection lies in their not attaching to the necessary and the impossible, as you have learned. And the deficiency, beyond which there is no deficiency, would be their attachment to them, leading to the annihilation of themselves and the annihilation of the exalted essence and the bringing into existence of a partner, incapability, and ignorance.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage explains why divine power and will do not attach to the necessary (wajib, i.e., God Himself) and the impossible (mustahil). The argument: power and will are attributes of effect (sifat ta'thir), and an effect requires non-existence prior to existence. The necessary (God) never had non-existence, and the impossible never accepts existence. Thus, attaching power or will to them would lead to absurdities: that would either be trying to produce what already exists (tahsil al-hasil) or changing essential realities (qalb al-haqa'iq). Therefore, it is a perfection that they do not attach to these, as attaching would imply deficiency such as God's own attributes being annihilated or coming into being.

Key Terms
الواجب
المستحيل
تحصيل الحاصل
قلب الحقائق
الكمال المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's power must extend to everything without exception, including making necessary things unnecessary or impossible things possible.
Correction: God's power does not extend to logical impossibilities or to what is already necessarily existent, because that would imply contradiction. This is not a limitation but a perfection of God's attributes.

Checkpoint
Why do power and will not attach to the necessary (wajib)?
A) Because the necessary is too great for them
B) Because the necessary never had non-existence
C) Because God does not need to will it
D) Because power and will are independent of the necessary

Key Takeaway
Divine power and will only attach to what is possible, not to the necessary or impossible, and this is a sign of God's perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself demanding from Allah that He change the past or undo something that has already happened, effectively asking for 'tahsil al-hasil'? What does this passage teach you about the perfection of not asking for what contradicts His attributes?
?
The passage says that if power attached to the impossible, it would lead to 'annihilation of the exalted essence'. How does this change the way you think about God's power? Do you truly accept that His power is defined by wisdom and logical coherence, not by sheer arbitrary force?
?
When you pray, do you sometimes ask for things that are inherently contradictory or impossible, expecting God to 'fix' them without considering whether they fit the category of the possible? How can you align your supplications with the truth that God's power only attaches to what can be an effect?

The Logical Ordering of Divine Power, Will, and Knowledge
Dardir states the logical ordering of power, will, and knowledge with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical ordering (tartīb) of Allah's attributes of power, will, and knowledge, and how this ordering governs the existence of things.

Core Idea
Divine power follows will, and will follows knowledge — a logical, not temporal, sequence.

In this passage, Imam Dardir explains a fundamental principle of Sunni theology: the divine attributes of power, will, and knowledge are related in a specific logical order. This order is not temporal (God does not 'learn' things in time) but logical: God's knowledge determines His will, and His will determines His power's effect. Understanding this ordering helps us grasp why some things happen and others do not, even when commanded.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow: the three attributes are listed in order.
The passage is dense – take your time.
2
Identify the ordering
Identify the three attributes (power, will, knowledge) and state how they are ordered. Dardir says: power follows will, will follows knowledge.
Think of it as: knowledge → will → power.
3
Reflect on the example
Dardir gives the example of a person who will persist in unbelief. Allah knows this, so He does not will the person's faith; hence faith does not occur despite the command. This illustrates the logical sequence in action.
This example shows how the ordering explains real-world outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم: أنَّ تعلق القدرةِ والإرادةِ والعِلم مترتب ، فتعلق القدرةِ تابع لتعلق الإرادة، وتعلق الإرادة تابع لتعلق العِلم، فلا يُوجد شَيئًا أو يعدِمُه إلا إذا أرادَه، ولا يُرِيدُه إلا إذا عَلِمَه، فما عَلِمَ أنه يكون أرادَ كَونَه ، ثُمَّ أبرَزَه على طبق الإرادة، وما علم أنه لا يكونُ فَلَمْ يُرِدْ كَونَه، فَلَمْ يَوجَدْ وإِنْ أَمَرَ بِه، كالإيمانِ مَمَنْ عَلِمَ اللَّهُ أَنه يستمِرُّ عَلَى الكُفْر حتى الموتِ. وإنها لم تتعلق القدرة والإرادة بالواجب والمستحيل؛ لأنهما لما كانا صفَتَي تأثير، ومن لازم الأَثَرِ وُجوده بعْدَ عدَم، لزِمَ أَنَّ ما لم يَقْبل العدَمَ أصْلا، وهو الواجب، وما يَقْبلُ الوُجودَ أَصْلًا، وهو المستحيلُ، لم يصِحَ أَن يكونَ أَثَرًا لها، وإلا لزِمَ تحصيل الحاصل وقلبُ الحقائقِ (۲) بصيرورة الواجب أو المستحيل جائزا، وهو تهافت لا يُعقَلُ. فالكمال المطلقُ في عدم تعلّقهما بالواجب والمستحيل؛ لما علمتَ ، والنقْصُ الذي ما بَعْدَه نقص تعلقها بهما، المؤدي ذلك إلى إعدامهما أنفُسِهما (۳) وإعدام الذاتِ العَليّةِ وإيجاد الشريك والعجز والجهل (٤)
Know that the attachment of power, will, and knowledge is ordered: the attachment of power follows the attachment of will, and the attachment of will follows the attachment of knowledge. So He does not bring something into existence or annihilate it unless He wills it, and He does not will it unless He knows it. So what He knows will be, He wills its existence, then He brings it forth in accordance with the will. And what He knows will not be, He does not will its existence, so it does not come into existence even if He commands it, like the faith of one whom Allah knows will persist in unbelief until death. And that power and will do not attach to the necessary (al-wājib) and the impossible (al-mustaḥīl); because since they are attributes of effect (ṣifata taʼthīr), and from the necessary concomitant of an effect is its existence after non-existence, it follows that what does not admit non-existence at all—which is the necessary—and what does not admit existence at all—which is the impossible—cannot be an effect of them; otherwise it would necessitate the acquisition of the already acquired (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) and the inversion of realities (qalb al-ḥaqāʼiq) by the necessary or the impossible becoming possible, which is an incomprehensible contradiction. So absolute perfection lies in their not attaching to the necessary and the impossible, for what you have known; and the deficiency beyond which there is no deficiency would be their attachment to them, leading to the annihilation of themselves and the annihilation of the exalted Essence and the bringing into existence of a partner, incapability ('ajz), and ignorance (jahl).
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage explains the logical ordering of divine attributes of power, will, and knowledge, and argues that power and will do not attach to the necessary or impossible. The author begins by stating that the attachments (ta'alluq) of power, will, and knowledge are ordered: power follows will, and will follows knowledge. This ordering is logical (in the mind), not temporal. Then he explains the sequence: nothing exists or is annihilated unless He wills it, and He only wills what He knows. For example, if Allah knows a person will persist in unbelief until death, He does not will that person's faith, so faith does not occur even if He commands it. Next, he argues that power and will do not attach to the necessary (wājib) or the impossible (mustaḥīl). The reason: power and will are attributes of effect (taʼthīr), and an effect must come after non-existence. The necessary never admits non-existence, and the impossible never admits existence, so they cannot be effects. If they were, it would imply 'acquiring the already obtained' (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) or 'inversion of realities' (qalb al-ḥaqāʼiq), which is contradictory. Finally, he concludes that it is absolute perfection that power and will do not attach to the necessary or impossible; if they did, it would lead to absurdities such as annihilating the attributes themselves, annihilating the divine essence, or bringing into existence a partner, incapability, or ignorance—all impossible.

Key Terms
تعلق (ta'alluq)
القدرة (al-qudra)
الإرادة (al-irāda)
العلم (al-ʿilm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ordering of power, will, and knowledge implies a temporal sequence where God learns things over time.
Correction: The ordering is logical, not temporal. God's knowledge is eternal and does not change; the sequence describes a relationship of dependency in terms of effect.

Checkpoint
According to Dardir, what is the logical order of the attachments of power, will, and knowledge?
A) Power follows will, and will follows knowledge.
B) Will follows power, and knowledge follows will.
C) Power follows knowledge, and knowledge follows will.
D) All three are independent and not ordered.

Key Takeaway
The attributes of divine power, will, and knowledge are logically ordered: knowledge determines will, and will determines power's effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the fact that God's knowledge determines what He wills affect your understanding of why some people you love persist in disbelief despite your efforts to guide them? In light of this lesson, what should be your true focus when calling others to faith?
?
If God's power only brings about what He wills, and He wills only what He knows, does this make your personal choices meaningless? How does this lesson actually give you confidence in God's perfect wisdom rather than fatalism?
?
The lesson says that even if God commands something, it only occurs if He wills it. How does this truth change the way you view your own sins — those you commit despite knowing God's command? What does it tell you about your need for God's mercy to will your obedience?

Why Divine Power and Will Do Not Apply to the Necessary and Impossible
Dardir proves that power and will do not attach to the necessary and impossible, and why this is a perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's power and will do not attach to the necessary (wājib) and impossible (mustaḥīl), and why this is a perfection.

Core Idea
Power and will are attributes of effect; they require non-existence prior to existence, which the necessary and impossible lack.

Imam Dardir now turns to a subtle but crucial point: divine power and will do not relate to the necessary (that which cannot not exist) or the impossible (that which cannot exist). Why? Because power and will produce effects, and an effect must come after non-existence. The necessary never undergoes non-existence, and the impossible never undergoes existence. If power and will attached to them, it would lead to absurdities like 'acquiring the already acquired' or 'inverting realities.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. This is a structured argument with a conclusion.
Pay attention to the logical connectives: 'because', 'so', 'otherwise'.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the terms 'necessary' (wājib), 'impossible' (mustaḥīl), 'effect' (athar), and 'attributes of effect' (ṣifata taʼthīr).
These are technical terms in kalam.
3
Understand the proof
The proof: Power and will are attributes of effect, meaning their effect must come into existence after non-existence. The necessary never has non-existence, the impossible never has existence. So they cannot be effects. If they were, it would involve a contradiction (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil or qalb al-ḥaqāʼiq).
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the perfection
Dardir concludes that it is absolute perfection that power and will do not attach to the necessary and impossible. If they did, it would lead to absurdities like annihilating God or bringing a partner.
This safeguards divine perfection.

Source Text

Know that the attachment of power, will, and knowledge is ordered: the attachment of power follows the attachment of will, and the attachment of will follows the attachment of knowledge. So He does not bring something into existence or annihilate it unless He wills it, and He does not will it unless He knows it. And that power and will do not attach to the necessary (al-wājib) and the impossible (al-mustaḥīl); because since they are attributes of effect (ṣifata taʼthīr), and from the necessary concomitant of an effect is its existence after non-existence, it follows that what does not admit non-existence at all—which is the necessary—and what does not admit existence at all—which is the impossible—cannot be an effect of them; otherwise it would necessitate the acquisition of the already acquired (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) and the inversion of realities (qalb al-ḥaqāʼiq) by the necessary or the impossible becoming possible, which is an incomprehensible contradiction.
